### 3. Financial-Statement-Modeling-An-Alternative-Approach

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] I've made an implicit assumption here that these purchases of non-current assets belong
[00:05 - 00:07] in the operating section.
[00:07 - 00:12] You could absolutely make the case that this could have been an investing activity.

[00:15 - 00:21] of the non-current assets here are intangible assets, well then purchases of intangible
[00:21 - 00:25] assets are an investing activity and then it would make sense to add a new line in here

[00:34 - 00:38] instead of here, moving it into here and then sort of re-subtotally.

[00:51 - 00:52] really care enough.
[00:52 - 00:56] It's just not a big enough line item to warrant all this concern, but I just want you to
[00:56 - 00:57] not get confused.
[00:57 - 01:00] This is, you could have assumed that this comes from investing activities and you would
[01:00 - 01:01] have been fine.

[01:07 - 01:11] happening here, whether you put it here or here becomes less important because you don't
[01:11 - 01:13] actually know the nature of these items.

[01:28 - 01:29] items.

[01:37 - 01:38] earnings.

[01:42 - 01:45] We're going to, when you're ready, move on to the next video and we're going to talk
[01:45 - 01:46] about retained earnings and model that.

### 4. Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Forecast-Concepts

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Forecast-Concepts/transcript/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Forecast-Concepts.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling\Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Forecast-Concepts\transcript\Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Forecast-Concepts.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:08] Alright, so we've got to the point in this model where we forecast as much of the income statement as we're going to forecast for a little while and now we turn to the balance sheet.
[00:08 - 00:17] So now there is a framework for forecasting the balance sheet that's a little bit different from forecasting the income statement and that's what I want to focus on here before we dive into the modeling.
[00:17 - 00:24] So we're going to start simple and then we're going to add advanced functionality to some balance sheet forecasts later on.
[00:24 - 00:35] Alright, I don't want you to lose the force for the trees. I want us to focus on the most meaningful elements of this which isn't just mechanics. It's, you know, what is the right way to forecast and then we're going to add a lot of bells and whistles you may encounter on the job.
[00:35 - 00:44] So we're going to start with working capital this cluster of line items that are tied to operating performance. Now how do we think about forecasting those?
[00:44 - 00:55] These should be forecast in line with operating forecasts. So as an example, let's assume there's no change in the company's expected day sales outstanding going forward.
[00:55 - 01:12] So with our case study for Apple, you know, if we're thinking that historically Apple has taken about 12 days to collect cash when it sells things on credit, we'll let's assume no change in that sort of relationship between cash and credit sales and how quickly it takes them to collect it.
[01:12 - 01:20] We would simply assume that as we forecast revenue growth, which we relied on JP Morgan to do, accounts receivable will grow as well commensrately.
[01:20 - 01:39] So the idea here is that the balance sheet, even though we're not explicitly going to sort of go to equity research and try to figure out what is JP Morgan saying about accounts receivable, what we want to do is recognize that the forecast for revenue is the indirect driver which we did receive, which we did get from JP Morgan.
[01:39 - 02:06] That is the fundamental driver of our base case on the balance sheet for accounts receivable. And that is again, assuming this big assumption is that I alluded to a little bit before, you know, we're going to do this to simplest way, we're going to assume no change in the company's day sales outstanding at first. And then later on in subsequent videos, we'll relax that assumption. But most of the time, you don't quite have a thesis on changing credit to cash relationships going forward. You just want to tie the accounts receivable line to the revenue line.

### 5. Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Schedules

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Schedules/transcript/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Schedules.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling\Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Schedules\transcript\Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balance-Sheet-Schedules.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] If you scroll down below the cash flow statement, we've added really four schedules.
[00:06 - 00:11] And one of the things that we sort of shortcut it originally is that when we built the
[00:11 - 00:16] balance sheet, we baked in forecasts to certain working capital items to really all these
[00:16 - 00:20] line-ups, we just did the forecasting directly on the balance sheet.
[00:20 - 00:25] The truth is that when you have enough information for any of these line-ups, you can really
[00:25 - 00:30] pull them out and create a schedule that then loops back into the balance sheet.
[00:30 - 00:35] Now, for things like accounts receivable, you can get away with baking it into the balance sheet
[00:35 - 00:38] for doing the forecast directly as we did here.
[00:38 - 00:42] But for certain line-ups, especially ones like property plan equilibrium where you have two things
[00:42 - 00:48] happening, capital expenditures and depreciation, it's always preferable to break it out into a schedule.
[00:48 - 00:53] In fact, many people argue it's better to break everything up into a schedule so that the balance
[00:53 - 00:59] sheet is just referencing from schedules, which is a reasonable argument when you have a reason to do so.
[00:59 - 01:02] We will later on break out even these line-ups into their own schedules.
[01:02 - 01:06] For now, I only want to do the work for where it really matters and where it's critical.
[01:06 - 01:11] And that's things like property plan equipment and things like retained earnings.
[01:11 - 01:13] So let's focus on that.

### 6. Financial-Statement-Modeling-Balancing-the-Model-Exercise

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:01 - 00:09] So at this point with the model complete, I'd like to take you through an exercise that is absolutely critical for model auditing and error-proofing your work.
[00:09 - 00:18] So one of the things that happens when you don't have the kind of step-by-step guidance that we provided in this course is that you build a model.
[00:18 - 00:24] And often it's a template that you repurposed or maybe sometimes you build a model from scratch.
[00:24 - 00:30] And you build the income statement, you build the balance sheet, you build the cash flow statement.

[00:37 - 00:40] And it tells you that you've done something wrong somewhere.
[00:40 - 00:48] Now this is one of those issues that can end up taking a ton of time for a lot of new analysts and associates trying to figure out where they went wrong.
[00:48 - 00:58] And so what I'd like to do is go through an exercise that tries to systematize the error-proofing process here to ensure that the model does ultimately balance.
[00:58 - 01:09] One thing that's critical to understand when models don't balance is that the reason models don't balance has to do with one very specific thing.
[01:09 - 01:20] And that is that the relationship between each line on the balance sheet is somehow not being reflected correctly on the cash flow statement.
[01:20 - 01:28] It really is a one to one thing. The only thing that makes models not balance is the relationship between the balance sheet and the cash flow statement.
[01:28 - 01:32] There's something wrong. And it could be anything that you've done in the model.
[01:32 - 01:35] The hard part is that it could be literally anything.
[01:35 - 01:41] But we know that the reason is there's something wrong between the relationship here and the relationship there.
[01:41 - 01:46] And that gives us a way of framework for really error-proofing.
[01:46 - 01:55] And the framework really involves looking at every line item and going down the list and making sure that each relationship is reflected correctly in both statements.
[01:55 - 02:03] So we're going to go through that process right now and I'm going to walk through a method that ensures that your model will ultimately balance.
[02:03 - 02:09] So the process starts with the first line after cash.
[02:09 - 02:13] So you skip cash because you deal with that at the very end.
[02:13 - 02:19] And the process involves really going down the list one by one on the balance sheet and finding, uh, finding errors.

[02:47 - 02:52] Off to the side here, you do your balance sheet check.
[02:52 - 03:02] And you say, okay, well, what is the expected impact that I would expect to see on the cash flow statement from the client and accounts receivable?
[03:02 - 03:07] And the answer is as accounts receivable go down, you expect to see an increase.
[03:07 - 03:15] And so you calculate that here and you store it and then you go and you check for it on the cash flow statement.
[03:15 - 03:29] Now, when you go to the cash flow statement, it's not always going to be that easy to see because as you recall, the way we built our working capital assets schedule, our cash flow statement and the way we calculated our working capital asset net impact is we actually lumped a bunch of things.
[03:29 - 03:36] So you have to look at those things and you say, okay, is the impact of this line accounts receivable?
[03:36 - 03:38] Is it being correctly reflected?

[03:42 - 03:55] But if it turns out when you look at this formula and you say, oh, you know, in fact, it is calculating it correctly, the impact of accounts receivable and inventory and other current assets together is being accurately reflected.
[03:55 - 04:05] Well, at that point, what you can do is you can not only check off this line that you've looked at it to your satisfaction, but that you can also check off this line.
[04:05 - 04:13] Now, you'll only be able to check off this line when all three items you verified the relationship is being correctly reflected in the cash flow statement.
[04:13 - 04:22] When you have checked off all of these line items, including cash at the very end, then your model is officially balanced.
[04:22 - 04:30] When you get the balance sheet and the cash flow statement, all the Xs, there's no way to do this correctly and have your model still be out of balance.

[04:42 - 04:50] It is the plug that every good financial statement model must have, which is like, hey, if we're going to model like a horrible scenario.

[00:00 - 00:09] Okay, in this video where you're going to talk about a calculation called the Kager, quite often it's used as calculated as a five year or a three year Kager.
[00:09 - 00:18] And the Kager basically looks at a sequence of financial results and let's look in this example we have an income statement for Apple that we forecast.
[00:18 - 00:27] The first three years here identified in blue have revenue of, you know, through 2016 to 2018 growing from $215 billion to $265 billion.
[00:27 - 00:34] And then you have five years of forecasts. The first year dips down a little bit and then the next year the next several years there's growth rates.
[00:34 - 00:37] And here are the revenue growth rates that are defining that.
[00:37 - 00:49] Well, what often makes its way into summary outputs in financial presentations is, well, you know, if I want just one statistic that's going to encapsulate what's happening over these five years.
[00:49 - 00:55] Almost like an average although it's not an average, it's not calculated like an average of the five years worth of growth rates.
[00:55 - 00:58] It's a compounded annual growth rate.
[00:58 - 01:00] What is the one rate?
[01:00 - 01:08] What is the one growth rate that if you seed into this year and grow over the next five years that'll get you to the three 11, that's what we want to see here.
[01:08 - 01:10] And that's how you calculate the five year Kager.
[01:10 - 01:13] So let's go ahead and do that. The math for this is fairly straightforward.
[01:13 - 01:17] You typically add a column at the end of the analysis.
[01:17 - 01:23] And you take the ending, the ending result divided by the first period result, right?
[01:23 - 01:33] And then once you do that, you're going to get something like, you know, there's a cumulative growth of whatever it is, like 17%.
[01:33 - 01:38] So you want to subtract that, you want to subtract a one from that.
[01:38 - 01:46] But because this 17% is happening over a five year period, you raise it to one over the fifth, right?
[01:46 - 01:54] And so the exponent here, before you subtract the one to get to the actual growth rate, is takes the number of four cast periods.
[01:54 - 02:00] And the key thing is you don't count the last actual year, take the four cast period, one, two, three, four, five.
[02:00 - 02:10] Now one way to make this sort of more dynamic, imagine if you added another four cast period, is you modify this to actually look at how many columns we have here.
[02:10 - 02:19] An easy way to make this dynamic is to have a formula here that, you know, says give me the number of columns in this range.
[02:19 - 02:23] And that makes it dynamic.
[02:23 - 02:27] I could have also done a count formula in here.
[02:27 - 02:30] And that would get me the same, would have got me the same result.
[02:30 - 02:31] Okay.
[02:31 - 02:32] So this is the way that we're going to do a kicker.
[02:32 - 02:35] And then it's just about copying and pasting the result.
[02:35 - 02:38] For other metrics that you might be interested in seeing Kakers for.

[02:44 - 02:50] In contrast with revenue, that's growing at 3.2%, and obviously the difference implies that there's some margin compression.
[02:50 - 02:58] In other words, the gross profit margins and the expense margins are a little worse in the out years than they were in the beginning.
[02:58 - 03:01] Obviously, revenue is outpacing the EBITDA Kager.
[03:01 - 03:02] Okay.
[03:02 - 03:07] So if you're ready to give that a shot yourself, this is some basic sort of Excel math here.

### 13. Financial-Statement-Modeling-Cash-Flow-Statement-Introduction

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Cash-Flow-Statement-Introduction/transcript/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Cash-Flow-Statement-Introduction.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling\Financial-Statement-Modeling-Cash-Flow-Statement-Introduction\transcript\Financial-Statement-Modeling-Cash-Flow-Statement-Introduction.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] We have now reached the point where we can turn to the cash flow statement.
[00:04 - 00:08] The cash flow statement is the final statement that we forecast.
[00:08 - 00:13] It's the most important in the sense that it is a reconciliation of everything that we've been doing.
[00:13 - 00:19] And specifically, the cash flow statement is a reconciliation of year-over-through changes in the balance sheet.
[00:19 - 00:24] We're not actually going to incorporate historical cash flow statement results.
[00:24 - 00:25] We don't need to.
[00:25 - 00:34] The forecast for the cash flow statements have already implicitly been made by virtue of us having forecast the balance sheet.
[00:34 - 00:41] And so you'll see that most financial statement models don't even bother putting in historical cash flow statement results in the cash flow statement.
[00:41 - 00:46] As we did with the income statement in the balance sheet, but rather instead, it is simply a forecast.
[00:46 - 00:52] And the forecast itself is just calculations that are referencing the things in the balance sheet that we've been talking about.
[00:53 - 00:58] So a couple of things to keep in mind before we dive into the modeling exercise.
[00:59 - 01:07] When models don't balance, in other words, when total assets do not equal liabilities plus equity on a forecast basis.
[01:07 - 01:13] That's almost often, almost always, due to silly mistakes in the cash flow statement.
[01:13 - 01:18] Sometimes they're not silly. Sometimes they're conceptual flaws, but quite often,
[01:18 - 01:23] they're due to silly mistakes like incorrectly putting this, forgetting to put a minus sign in front of
[01:23 - 01:26] CapEx to show that it's in outflow or things of that nature.
[01:27 - 01:33] Now the construction of a cash flow statement of a correct cash flow statement in a three-statement financial statement model
[01:34 - 01:38] requires a deep understanding of how the three financial statements actually interrelate.
[01:39 - 01:47] So what I'd like to just point out before we get into this is that understanding the interrelationship of the three financial statements
[01:47 - 01:55] is so critical to doing this step is that we've included specific lessons from the accounting crash course
[01:55 - 02:00] specific to the cash flow statement. And I want you to go through those lessons if you haven't already
[02:00 - 02:06] or are not quite comfortable with the connections between the cash flow statement and the income statement
[02:06 - 02:11] and the balance sheet. If you are comfortable or you're feeling adventurous, you don't have to go through
[02:11 - 02:16] the accounting crash course videos that have included directly after this video and jump immediately
[02:16 - 02:21] into the modeling videos. But if you want to take it slow, you're feeling like you're not
[02:21 - 02:26] necessarily sure how the cash flow statement relates conceptually to the other two financial statements
[02:26 - 02:32] definitely invest the time if you haven't already looking through those other videos.

[01:56 - 02:02] So we're moving away from JP Morgan now in fact really as you'll see we this is really all we rely on for JP Morgan
[02:02 - 02:05] You can build an entire model with just a few core
[02:05 - 02:09] Drivers and they're driving the rest of it. So let's take a look what that means

[00:00 - 00:04] So here we've created a section called working capital where we've basically said,
[00:04 - 00:07] okay, well, let's look at the historical accounts receivable, right?
[00:07 - 00:12] Which we've pulled from the balance sheet and let's forecast this using a regular roll forward.
[00:12 - 00:15] And so the regular roll forward, as you now know from prior videos,
[00:15 - 00:20] is starts with the prior year's end of period balance and you grow to the next period.
[00:21 - 00:24] And then whatever the end of period balance is going to be,
[00:24 - 00:27] you subtract it from the beginning of period balance and that's going to be the delta.
[00:27 - 00:30] And now what we're going to focus on is how are we going to do this forecast?
[00:30 - 00:32] We're not just going to simply grow with revenue.
[00:32 - 00:34] It's going to be one of the options.
[00:34 - 00:38] But we are going to also give the users the option of saying something else.

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_Financial_Statement_Modeling/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Retained-Earnings-Roll-Forward-Concepts/transcript/Financial-Statement-Modeling-Retained-Earnings-Roll-Forward-Concepts.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] The next schedule that's going to require a little bit more care similar to property plan equipment,
[00:04 - 00:08] there's more than just one moving part in here, is the retained earnings roll forward, right?
[00:08 - 00:14] We start with last year's retained earnings, that's the beginning of period balance for the current year,
[00:14 - 00:18] and then whatever happens during the year, that raises retained earnings in terms of net income,
[00:18 - 00:23] right? So net income raises retained earnings, since we're forecasting net income from the income
[00:23 - 00:27] statement, that's coming straight from the income statement, right? So that's going to increase
[00:27 - 00:32] our schedule. Well, what lowers retained earnings? Well, the things that lower retained earnings
[00:32 - 00:37] typically are just common and prefer dividends during the year, and Apple, as we'll see shortly,
[00:37 - 00:42] as well, we've already seen when we put in our historical results, it issues a ton of common
[00:42 - 00:47] dividends, right? So this is going to be significant. It's going to, you know, it's going to reduce
[00:47 - 00:54] retained earnings after net income increases it. Now, for Apple, the retained earnings bucket, the way
[00:54 - 00:59] they account for things, also includes treasury share buybacks. Most companies break this out into
[00:59 - 01:06] a separate treasury stock account, but in Apple, there's a third element that we need to factor in,
[01:06 - 01:11] which reduces retained earnings, as opposed to just reducing a contra treasury stock account,
[01:11 - 01:15] as you would see for most companies. No big deal, but this is why you see retained earnings for
[01:15 - 01:21] Apple over time actually declining, whereas for a company like Apple, you would actually expect
[01:21 - 01:27] it's retained earnings to keep growing. These sort of two together, we can think of these items
[01:27 - 01:32] together as kind of a redistribution to shareholders of income. And if you're, if you're giving back
[01:32 - 01:36] more than the net income, you're generating, you're going to lower your retained earnings from one
[01:36 - 01:38] year to the next.

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] In this video, we finally introduced the cash flow statement.
[00:04 - 00:08] Up till now, we've gone through all the major line items of the income statement.
[00:08 - 00:11] Then we went through the balance sheet and talked a lot about how the balance sheet
[00:11 - 00:13] is interrelated to the income statement.
[00:13 - 00:17] But the very last exercise that we did in the prior video that lemonade stand exercise
[00:17 - 00:22] where you needed to complete an income statement and an end of period balance sheet.
[00:22 - 00:26] One of the things that we realized is that the hardest thing to do is actually to figure out
[00:26 - 00:28] what the cash balance is at the end of the period.
[00:28 - 00:33] The cash flow statement really is about trying to reconcile the income statement to what's
[00:33 - 00:36] going on from a cash profitability standpoint.
[00:36 - 00:40] So while the income statement is very useful because it attempts to provide insight to
[00:40 - 00:43] a company's true profitability using accrual accounting.
[00:43 - 00:46] And that matches revenue with corresponding expenses.
[00:46 - 00:51] It also allows for and requires management guide and judgment about, say, what is the useful
[00:51 - 00:53] life of the asset that we're depreciating?
[00:53 - 00:56] What is the value of inventory?
[00:56 - 00:58] Should we use life for or FIFO?
[00:58 - 01:04] What is the revenue recognition assumptions that we should incorporate to various multiple
[01:04 - 01:06] deliverables and things like that nature?
[01:06 - 01:10] All of those assumptions and judgment can have material impacts on profitability.
[01:10 - 01:14] And thus, it can be potentially misleading in terms of what's going on from a company,
[01:14 - 01:22] liquidity standpoint and from a darker perspective, it can also be prone to manipulation.
[01:22 - 01:26] So imagine a scenario where a company may show high accrual based profitability, your
[01:26 - 01:31] income statement may show high profitability, but it could very easily be running out of cash
[01:31 - 01:36] because significant revenues that were recognized were actually non-cash.
[01:36 - 01:42] There were sold on credit and the folks that bought it on credit aren't expected to pay.
[01:42 - 01:46] Imagine conversely, a company that from an accrual accounting standpoint may show lower
[01:46 - 01:50] negative profitability on the income statement, but it's generating a ton of cash during
[01:50 - 01:55] the period because the major expense that was recognized was this non-cash depreciation
[01:55 - 01:57] immunization expense.
[01:57 - 02:00] So these are the limitations of the income statement.
[02:00 - 02:04] It's a fundamental limitation of recrual accounting that by virtue of what it tries to do,
[02:04 - 02:09] which is to show true profitability by matching expenses with revenue, to give investors
[02:09 - 02:12] a better picture of what's actually going on during the year.
[02:12 - 02:19] What ends up happening is that there's a disconnect from what's actually happening to cash.
[02:19 - 02:23] And so the way that we resolve that disconnect is that along with the balance sheet and the
[02:23 - 02:31] income statement, the cash flow statement becomes a tool that enables investors to understand
[02:31 - 02:32] what happened to cash.
[02:32 - 02:39] It's a required financial statement and it reconciles net income accounting based net income
[02:39 - 02:44] to the company's actual change in cash balances over the particular period in time where
[02:44 - 02:47] we're looking at the cash flow statements are usually either a quarter for the queues for
[02:47 - 02:50] the ten queues or a year for the ten kids.
[02:50 - 02:55] And it's because of the dual, dual, the important roles of the income statement and the
[02:55 - 03:02] cash flow statement that they both need to be used and fully understood together by analysts.
[03:02 - 03:05] Looking at the income statement and ignoring the cash flow statement is very risky because
[03:05 - 03:07] of some of the examples we just talked about.
[03:07 - 03:11] But the other side of that is true as well.
[03:11 - 03:14] Some folks say, well, what's the point of the accrual concept income statement?
[03:14 - 03:17] We might as well just use the cash flow statement and focus only on that.
[03:17 - 03:20] And you do that at your own peril as well because simply looking at the cash flow statement
[03:20 - 03:26] doesn't fully shed light on what's happening from a core profitability standpoint.
[03:26 - 03:32] We gave the example of the Boeing selling airplane contracts and these are long-term
[03:32 - 03:33] in nature.
[03:33 - 03:38] And so if you were looking at it on the earlier periods when Boeing was just buying
[03:38 - 03:42] all the equipment and all the expenses are going to make up the inventory, then you
[03:42 - 03:44] chose negative cash outflows.
[03:44 - 03:49] But then that would be misleading because the company, it may be doing that to service
[03:49 - 03:55] and generate very profitable business over the next several years.
[03:55 - 04:00] So under both US gap and IFRS, companies actually have two options for how they report
[04:00 - 04:02] the cash flow statement.
[04:02 - 04:05] The first approach is called the direct method and the second approach is called the indirect
[04:05 - 04:06] method.
[04:06 - 04:10] Since indirect method is what virtually all choose, that's the one we're going to focus
[04:10 - 04:11] on.
[04:11 - 04:16] Both approaches do require cash flows to be classified into three components.
[04:16 - 04:22] Cash from operations, so this is the inflows and inflows that's related solely or fundamentally
[04:22 - 04:27] to the operations of your business, then cash from investing activities, which reflects
[04:27 - 04:33] any major capital expenditures, asset sales or purchases, and lastly cash from financing
[04:33 - 04:37] activities, which reflects any new borrowing paid out of debt.
[04:37 - 04:41] The new issuance of stock, the sharee purchases, the issuance of debt.
[04:41 - 04:49] So under FASB, under US gap and IFRS, there's a separation of three types of activities.
[04:49 - 04:54] Now that also creates some gray areas, by the way, because some activities could be classified
[04:54 - 04:59] as operational activities, but they could also be classified as investing activities.
[04:59 - 05:02] And this creates a gray zone as well, but ultimately the cash flow statement is much more
[05:02 - 05:06] straightforward because once you define the classifications, it's just cash and versus
[05:06 - 05:07] cash out.

[05:11 - 05:14] Cash from operations is the most important section from an analytical standpoint, because
[05:14 - 05:17] it measures how the business is actually doing operationally.
[05:17 - 05:21] And it's the one where there's this difference between the indirect and the direct method.

[05:27 - 05:31] And it basically says start with accounting net income.
[05:31 - 05:34] It's the indirect method because it arrives at cash from operations indirectly.
[05:34 - 05:39] It doesn't just say, okay, how much cash receipts that we get minus cash disbursements,
[05:39 - 05:42] which is what the indirect method is.

[05:43 - 05:48] And it says start with net income, accounting based accrual net income, and make a whole
[05:48 - 05:54] bunch of adjustments to convert that accrual based net income into cash flows from operations.
[05:54 - 06:01] Basically eliminate all the non-cash items that were skewing net income because it was
[06:01 - 06:06] a cruel base to actually arrive at cash true cash flow from operations.

[06:42 - 06:43] This is a fairly common representation.
[06:43 - 06:48] We have their income statement here and their bottom line is their net income or net earnings.
[06:48 - 06:52] So in 2013, they generated $11.4 billion in net income.

[07:02 - 07:03] about.

[07:20 - 07:25] net income of $11.4 billion, but cash in my pocket profits was actually $14.8 billion.
[07:25 - 07:26] So there's a little bit of a difference.

[07:32 - 07:33] than net income.

[07:49 - 07:52] operations line.

[08:04 - 08:05] income.

[08:10 - 08:14] selling general and administrative and cost of product sold, buried within these large

[08:39 - 08:48] case here, the biggest adjustment to get from accounting profits to non-cash profit.

[08:54 - 08:57] of balance sheet line items called working capital items.
[08:57 - 09:04] So current assets like accounts receivable, inventory, prepaid expenses are called working

[09:05 - 09:10] Current liabilities like accounts payable, crude expenses, deferred revenue are called
[09:10 - 09:13] working capital liabilities.
[09:13 - 09:17] Both represent assets and liabilities that are tied up in the ordinary course of operations,
[09:17 - 09:23] which is why we classify their cash impact under cash from operations, as opposed to cash
[09:23 - 09:25] from investing or financing.

[10:21 - 10:25] Now the impact on the cash flow statement of changes in working capital assets and liabilities
[10:25 - 10:30] is an example of a broader accounting concept that we have been discussing up till now.
[10:30 - 10:36] And that is that when assets are increasing, that represents a usage of funds or a cash
[10:36 - 10:37] outflow.
[10:37 - 10:43] For example, a company buys inventory with cash.
[10:43 - 10:50] The inventory is going up and that increase had to be funded via cash.
[10:50 - 10:54] Because in liability and equity represents a source of funds or a cash inflow.
[10:54 - 10:58] If a company issued stock or debt, they get cash in return.
[10:58 - 11:01] The company would get cash in return, so that becomes a source of funds.
[11:01 - 11:05] And so whenever you're working through a cash flow statement, it's actually very important
[11:05 - 11:10] to remember a broad rule, which is increases in assets, represent a use of funds or a cash
[11:10 - 11:16] outflow, whereas increases in liability and equity represent a source of funds.
[11:16 - 11:20] The next section that we want to just address is other items.
[11:20 - 11:24] You'll often see in cash flow operations, certain other things, other things that make
[11:24 - 11:27] the list in cash flow operations are the things like asset write downs.

[11:32 - 11:36] down specifically that lemon squeezer that we thought wasn't working anymore.
[11:36 - 11:41] Although that reduced net income, it certainly doesn't have a cash impact.
[11:41 - 11:44] And so we need to add that back to get to cash flow operations and the same is true
[11:44 - 11:49] for any type of impairment, like a goodwill impairment or an inventory write down.
[11:49 - 11:54] Any sort of accounting write down is going to need to get added back from net income
[11:54 - 11:55] to get to cash in my pocket.

### 50. Financial-Statement-Modeling-Roll-Forward-Concept

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] So far we have forecast line items directly in the balance sheet.
[00:03 - 00:10] For example, accounts receivable, we simply forecasted by connecting the prior period accounts receivable
[00:11 - 00:16] to the revenue growth rate and forecasting it that way right on the face of the balance sheet.
[00:16 - 00:20] Now for more complex line items, a separate schedule is sometimes warranted.
[00:20 - 00:26] And the way we call this, there's a sort of a way to build a schedule for more complicated line
[00:27 - 00:32] and that method is called the rule forward. It's the most common way to organize these schedules.
[00:32 - 00:37] And all the only thing a rule forward means is you start with the last historical year,
[00:37 - 00:44] the beginning of period balance, which is really last year's previous year's end of period balance.
[00:44 - 00:51] And then you add any increases, right? So that gets you, let's say you started at 100 for a line item,
[00:51 - 00:58] and you think about, well, what makes that line item go up? Let's say you added something to that
[00:58 - 01:05] line item, you added $40, $40 said that line item. So that gets you to, you know, $140. So this is,
[01:05 - 01:11] you know, it rolls in addition and increase. And then you subtract, let's say you ended up
[01:11 - 01:15] reducing that line item by 20 during the year as well, right? So there may be moving things,
[01:15 - 01:19] we're going to get to retain earnings in a second and we can see imagine if we bought
[01:19 - 01:26] capital expenditures and reduced it by depreciation. Well, that gets you to a net of sort of 120,
[01:26 - 01:32] right? You started at 100, you added 40, then you subtract the 20, you got down to 120 here.
[01:32 - 01:37] So this is kind of where you end up. But the framework is that you start at the beginning of
[01:37 - 01:41] period, which is really last year's end of period balance, you have additions, subtractions,
[01:41 - 01:45] and then you get to the end of period balance. And that's the way to think about sort of
[01:45 - 01:51] mechanically building this. Another term for this is base, roll forward or base. Those terms
[01:51 - 02:01] are used interchangeably. And, and the reason why it's called base is you start with beginning,
[02:01 - 02:07] right? That's this box. And then you have additions. And that's this plus. And then you have
[02:07 - 02:20] subtractions. And then at the end, you have your end of period, right? So base or roll forward.
